193700788X|1.0|For me it's been mighty difficult framing a review on one single book in a long running series. I just can't stop referring back to the earlier books and its making me agitated. One would think each book of any series would be building toward a resounding end where the protagonist's and other beloved characters' problems would be resolved for the better. But this is not the case with the Southern Vampire Mysteries (SVM) series. Or is it the Sookie Stackhouse Novels? Or is it True Blood Books? I can't seem to get a straight answer on this. Another thing no one can answer is what was the intended purpose of this long running series.<br />Here's a little background, I was introduced to the SVM series after not liking True Blood and hearing that the books were much better. In a matter of weeks, I was hooked under the ruse that the vampires represented a marginalized group in this fictitious world and the protagonist, Sookie Stackhouse, was the marginalized telepath bridging the divide, at least in Bon Temps, Louisiana. She was also good at connecting the dots and courageous in the face of adversity. The writing, especially in the earlier books, is fast pace with an easy storytelling style. The authors assumed voice could at times be quaint but funny; so much, so that I let the inconsistencies here and there slide. Trusting that it all would be explained at the end. At the culmination of an over a decade long investment that wouldn't dream of leaving the author, her characters, and the fans walking away from the series with hurt feelings, dashed expectations, and disillusionment about the redeeming value of a 13 book series.<br />I have questioned, and thought, and rethought, and reread and I've come to the same conclusion every time. Charlaine Harris never truly cared for this story and this fiasco of an ending is because the SVM series is a GIMMICK - a TRICK - a PLOY - a STUNT - to:<br />A. Advance careers; and<br />B. Make money off of the unsuspecting VERMIN - Me<br/>Since learning the spoilers from Dead Ever After and interacting with the fandom (something I've never done), I've come to understand that it really didn't matter whether I read:<br />Dead Until Dark,<br />Living Dead In Dallas,<br />Club Dead,<br />Dead To The World,<br />Dead As A Doornail,<br />Definitely Dead,<br />All Together Dead,<br />And yes, I am going to type each title out to illustrate my frustration with this entire series from beginning to end. Now back to the still ever growing list of books I purchased and read:<br />From Dead to Worse,<br />Dead And Gone,<br />Dead In The Family,<br />Dead Reckoning,<br />Deadlocked,<br />Dead Ever After<br />Count them, and then recount them again. God help me I just can't finish the last book in this series knowing that my initial investment didn't lay the breadcrumbs to the ending.  I can't continue to read when what any of the characters said, did, or felt, Charlaine Harris conveniently forgets rules, conversations, and characterization whenever it suits her needs. Just as I began to question her sense, I learn there are a number of books written on the side that were important to the series, you see. Yes, all you unsuspecting souls that wear many hats in life and know that time and money are in short supply. There are a ton of `Novellas' written by the author that have plot driving information contained within - Oh the deviousness. What you didn't know?! No. The novellas or the Sam Side Stories (SSS) as I've come to call them are actually important to the outcome of the series. But wait, wouldn't it have been good form, and honest, for readers to have known the importance of these SSS Novellas in their favorite series. Should they have read?:<br />If I Had A Hammer, Small Town Wedding, and the other Novellas I wasn't made to care about, and now that the series is over, they matter. Man that's some honest dealing, I tell ya. There are hundreds if not thousands of other readers that were in the dark like me.<br />Dearies not only is the SVM series a gimmick it is also deceptive. The SSS story lines ought to have been added to the main books if they were so important to the outcome of the series.  If there were words, actions, and/or hints that affected the protagonist life, and had some bearing on the ending, then lengthen the body of the 12 books, or add more books to the original series. (Shoot we were willing to read 13 books, why would we stop there?)<br />Or if all else fails, here's a better idea, stick to the rules and the history of the original series to remain honest with the fans. See I enjoy paranormal romance because it's an escape and ignites my imagination. In fantasy and make believe the skies the limit, right?<br />But folks honesty in the SVM is not the best policy - the name of the series conveniently changed to fit the mood of the author over time. At first it was a series about a marginalized telepath meeting, working and loving marginalized vampires that had conveniently \\"come out of the coffin\\" (vampire increased popularity in romantic fiction). After that was used to the point of being bothersome for Ms. Harris, it became a series about supernatural mysteries solved by the protagonist alone with the vampires this time being marginalized by the author. Then at the end, the story is about Sookie Stackhouse and her journey or lack thereof. The series changed focused three times over the years. As a consequence it became over marketed as it tried to be everything and anything to attract readers = $.<br />1. Hey if you like paranormal and vampire books, then the Southern Vampire Mystery series is for you. Don't listen to the author when she says in interviews that she thinks it's stupid to date vampires - as if it's actually a real choice women have. (I still can't believe she said this about her own protagonist and actually meant it. No wonder the ending is so uninspiring.)<br />2.  Hey if you like romance, then the Southern Vampire Mystery series isfor you. Don't listen to the author when she says she doesn't write romance, she's just being coy. (Yes, listen to the author and you should disregard the books altogether, particularly the ones that have any romantic situations or themes, just read Dead Until Dark and Dead Ever After.)<br />3.  Hey if you like a fantasy with a social conscious twist, then the Southern Vampire Mystery series...well, you get the idea.<br />In reality the SVM series was never any of the things it was marketed to be but a GIMMICK to entice the VERMIN - the unsuspecting Paranormal Romance fans to read Charlaine Harris' novels. A gimmick to attract people like me who see no problem with human vampire `relations' in a fantasy that takes place in a small town during modern times, people like me that like happy endings or traceable surprises, and people like me that appreciate the subtext of social conscience in a light read.  But instead of the above SVM was a stunt, and strategically planned and played out with the consent of Charlaine Harris. Then finally after 15 years and 13 books, Ms. Harris decides that she needs to be true to herself and write a fitting ending to a convoluted, deceptive and money grabbing series. And that is how Dead Ever After was conceived and why it reads so horribly.<br />Now for those of you still supporting Charlaine Harris and perceive no problem in her career stunt, I can only say to each their own. But the online fan interaction, and chats at forums and book signings, was asinine behavior on her part. Perhaps it would not have been a problem if Ms. Harris didn't use that time to correct, change, or alter the series to fix her poorly done writing. To this day, I can't read anything related to SVM without someone adding this precursor \\"Well Charlaine Harris did say....\\" Excuse me but I thought what only mattered was what Charlaine Harris wrote in the 12 proceeding books.<br />I, like others, have never attended Ms. Harris' book signings and forums. There areplenty of interviews online and in print that I have never seen nor read. Ever considered that some people live too far to attend a signing or can't even afford the trip, or simply don't have the time to do so. Some live in rural communities where libraries are far away, or closed down, and the internet is a luxury to afford. Ever consider that the one book a year was all some people could afford?<br />I am not a personal fan of Charlaine Harris. I was a fan of the characters she created, and I enjoyed catching up with them over the 6 years of reading the series. I wouldn't know about all the internet talk and the SSS books. All of which would have been fine if it were not being used to tell the story the author couldn`t show; just bad form all around, Charlaine.<br />So folks I end this series with not so happy thoughts about it. Like the major characters in Dead Ever After, I feel used and short changed. Both of which are feelings that are not usually associated with paranormal romance. But considering all I've learned over the last two weeks I was never supposed to feel good about this series. Because although it was conceived to entice readers like me, in actuality the author thinks that I'm VERMIN - a pest and a nuisance. Charlaine Harris has neither respect for me as a reader nor as a consumer. It isn't enough that I purchased and promoted her series for over 6 years. It isn't enough that she gained legends of fans that eerily hold on to her every word. And it isn't enough that for 15 years she has grown in stature and income. Nothing, not even love, is enough for her to stop seeing the fans and readers as VERMIN.<br />With that said Ms. Harris, consider yourself now bug free.<br />One Star, not even a bookshelf positioned series.

B00CMKPNBC|5.0|French folk/jazz singer extraordinaire, Zaz simply hits it out of the park on her second studio album. I've followed Zaz for years, and must admit that I was scared that I might have over-hyped this album to myself. It's rare that a singer can maturely evolve their sound without losing their special essence. To my utter delight, there isn't a sophomore slump in sight on this album. This album samples a broader range of music genres than Zaz's self-titled debut, yet Zaz's soulful and emotive singing is front and center at all times.<br /><br />Musically wise, the first thing that struck me--was that this album was much more guitar driven as opposed to piano driven. I must admit that I miss the driving piano and think in general that you can hear much more emotion in a piano than from a guitar, but the fact of the matter is--these new more guitar focused songs are just flat out awesome. \\"La lessive\\" is subtle and controlled, yet keeps popping back into my head. I think I miss the more piano like songs, as they remind me a bit more of Edith Piaf, but this is a fresher sound for Zaz. While she can nail the sultry powerful cabaret siren, the wandering street minstrel seems to be a bit truer to who Zaz is as a person, and I think it shows in her music as well.<br /><br />\\"Si je perds\\" however is a haunting piano piece that just flows from the soul. Zaz is practically speaking, her tone is so light and soft yet the sorrow in her voice just cuts through. \\"Si\\" is another standout for me in a similar manner as \\"Si je perds\\" yet is a bit more forceful and gritty. Now, while Zaz's slower songs tend to be my favorites, I don't want to give the erroneous impression that this album is full of sad slow songs. Quite the contrary, about half of them are much more upbeat and incorporate a wide range of musical instruments like the cello, accordion and even some that I can't quite identify. \\"Nous debout\\" is an immediate standout that will definitely make you want to tap your toes and probably attempt to sing along as well.<br /><br />I do want to add that I don't speak or understand any French at all. Occasionally I hear a word that I may or may not recognize, but I certainly don't have any comprehension of what the songs are about unless I've previously looked the lyrics up. I write this to emphasize that Zaz simply makes music that transcends language. I would highly encourage anyone who enjoys Edith Piaf, Constance Amiot or even someone like Colbie Caillat to check out Zaz. Understanding of French not required to enjoy the beautiful and memorable experience that Zaz creates.robably attempt to sing along as well.<br /><br />I do want to add that I don't speak or understand any French at all. B00919V32K|5.0|Why did I stop listening to Zappa in the early 80's?  Because the band that he had assembled after the demise of the Underwood/Brock/Thompson/Fowler based bands of the 70's just didn't match up to the level of genius, in my own small opinion.  Joe's Garage?  Did you hear that fart sound?  Well, that was me giving my review of the domages.  For some reason, chemistry?, changing times?, mistakes?, the band that did most of the music in the early 80's just did not work , once again in my own opinion.  Consequently, I stopped listening to Zappa.  Then, Zappa changed his band once again and had actually achieved a goal with this new band that he had sought for decades.  Yes, decades.  The late 80's band actually was the Greatest Band That I Never Heard in my life.  His goal, often completely unrecognized, was the performance of LIVE MUSIC WITHOUT THE USE OF OVERDUBS.  Secretly, Zappa hated overdubs.  He wanted you to hear it there, while you were there.  He did not want to take it back to the Studios Of Muffin to add later toots and thumps and plucks.  If it needed more music , then he considered it almost a failure.  BUT, with this album, The Best Band You Never Heard In Your Life, Frank achieved one of his dreams(I think), that is to play music, live music with the entire sound created there.  Folks, he did it.  This album is tight with tallent, teamwork and intent.  It rocks, it jazzes, and it classics, just like Frank.  Supposedly &#34;Make a Jazz Noise Here&#34; and One Other 90's Album were all assembled from shows made with this accomplished band.  This is a great album.  4 out of 4 stars.  Johnny P.

0312330529|4.0|The story is irresistible: an escaped convict from Australia finds redemption in India.  The broad story line is familiar to readers of Dominique Lapierre's \\"City of Joy\\" and similar stories that juxtaposes a rather imperfect western characters with the Indian tapestry to create a deep, vibrant and multi-hued portrait.  Invariably, the rhythms of India work their magic on the westerner.<br /><br />Much of this is there in \\"Shantaram\\", a story told in the first person by Gregory David Roberts in an autobiographical manner.  The zeitgeist of the novel is freedom.  Freedom to be true to yourself, freedom to love, freedom to pursue liberty, and freedom from being pursued by the long arm of the law.  The first half of the story is simply enchanting as the reader is immersed in Mr. Roberts' world of escaping the Australian prison and getting seemingly lost in the teeming masses of India.  Mr. Roberts learns the ubiquitous and idiosyncratic Indian head wiggle and is proud to have mastered it.  He learns some Hindi and Marathi, the latter being the language of Mumbai, which he calls home.  The ancillary characters created by Mr. Roberts that help him get acclimated to India are very well sketched out.  Prabhaker, his guide that becomes a dear friend; Johnny Cigar, a name formed by joining words from two unrelated street posters; Karla, Mr. Roberts' muse; Didier, a french expat lounging in India; Abdel Khader Khan, the intellectual but deadly mafia don; Abdullah, an Iranian expat running from the Iran-Iraq war (this is the 1980's) who becomes a blood brother to Mr. Roberts, and many more.<br /><br />Armed with a comprehensive first-aid kit and pedantic knowledge gained from a class in Australia that taught him the basics of nursing, Mr. Roberts becomes a veritable doctor to the folks of the \\"zhopadpatti\\" (the slum) where he settles.  Away from the arm of the law, he finds refuge and dignity by treating simple folks of rat bites, dysentery, diarrhea and similar ailments.  Butof course, these are not enough to make ends meet, so he arranges the rendezvous of westerners and locals so the former parts with money and the latter parts with quality marijuana.  Mr. Roberts gets a small cut from the transaction.  His wheeling-dealing and foolhardy bravery (there isn't a fight that Mr. Roberts runs away from) gets him noticed by the local mafia don and soon Mr. Roberts moves to the big leagues.  He loves, looses his love, makes friends, looses friends, participates in the Afghan war (Afghanistan and Russia are embraced in a deadly embrace) to search for the meaning of life, gets imprisoned in the infamous Arthur Road Jail in Mumbai, and ultimately realizes that freedom is elusive.<br /><br />The novel is huge --- more than 900 pages.  The first half is simply enthralling.  The second half starts feeling a bit lugubrious and meanders on repeating the same platitudes of lost love and freedom that the author talked about earlier.  Fortunately, all this changes when Mr. Roberts describes his time in Arthur Road prison; that part redeems the second half.  The story takes on a tragic turn after he is released from prison.  As if to atone for his myriad losses, Mr. Roberts follows don Khan to Afghanistan, who ironically becomes completely ineffectual in his home environment.  Mr. Roberts returns to India a changed man, having learned that freedom is elusive.  One is free to pursue his destiny, but the freedom to do so shackle other aspects of one's life in ways that can be neither forseen nor foretold.<br /><br />Despite the uneven pacing in the second half, I think this book is simply marvellous.  The tale is captivating, the characters are excellent and the story telling aptitude of Mr. Roberts, for the most part, is great. (April 2013).

1400069742|3.0|One of the principal villains in this book is Charles Lindbergh. From the book we learn that Lindbergh was a lousy husband, an indifferent father,politcally naive and not even a particularly good pilot. I will not comment further on the author's assertions about Lindbergh's private life, but the facts surrounding his epic flight from New York to Paris are a matter of record.<br />The author dismisses it early in the book, claiming that it was a mere \\" stunt \\". This is hardly borne out by the facts.<br />Well aware of the risk he was taking, Lindbergh methodically planned his flight every day for months. The Spirit of St. Louis was purpose built expressly for this flight at a cost of $ 10,000, a large sum of money in 1927. The airplane was \\" stripped down\\" only in the sense that it did not contain a single component or piece of equipment that was not absolutely necessary to accomplish it's proposed flight.Lindbergh wrote a memorable account of the flight in 1953. Titled \\" The Spirit of St. Louis \\", it won a Pulitzer Prize the following year and explains in detail his reasoning for decisions not to include a parachute or a radio, among other things. Also, Lindbergh didn't\\" navigate by the stars, something he wasn't very familiar with .\\" He used dead reckoning, and did it so well that his landfall on the Irish coast was within a mile or two of his intended goal. A modern pilot using a GPS couldn't have done much better.<br />For a number of years, Charles Lindbergh engaged regularly in some of the most high risk flying ever undertaken and walked away without a scratch. This isn't the resume of a stunt flyer. His place in the history of aviation is secure, and will continue to be recognized long after this book has gone out of print.<br />Regarding Franklin Roosevelt, it would appear from this book that he was a knave - a second or third rate political operative who, without widespread political support, dithered, waffled, and pursued private vendettas  while his nationdrifted inexorably toward war.This is certainly not consistent with other accounts of his presidency I've read.<br />I had high expectations for this book. Ms. Olson is the co - author of \\"A Question of Honor\\", a marvelous book that remains one of my favorites on the subject of the Second World War.This book just isn't in the same class, in my opinion.

B009PHRIAE|5.0|These are re-releases of Brueggen's Telefunken recordings. The weakest performances on this set are Frans Brueggen's collaboration with the Concentus Musicus of Vienna on Telemann concertos. The strongest piece is a reworked Bach trio sonata for viola da gamba, recast as a haunting sonata for two flutes, again with the Concentus Musicus. The Telemann concertos are merely good. The Bach is mesmerizing.<br /><br />The bulk of this set consists of performances by three people, Brueggen, Anner Bylsma on the cello, and Gustav Leonhardt. This trio played together for decades. They were always good, from the Telemann sonatas of the early 60s to the much later French sonatas, you also get to hear a progression of playing style, instruments and recording engineering. The French Sonatas disc is one of the absolute gems of the set.<br /><br />Then there are the unaccompanied pieces by composers ranging from the early, van Eyck, to the relatively late, Telemann. These remain the definitive performances.<br /><br />For some variety, you get English consort music, and the occasional concerto. Brueggen also teams up with his students Walter van Hauve and Kees Boeke for some lovely duets and trios.<br /><br />Should you buy this set? If you love the recorder, you will buy the set. If you love the instrument, you already know all about Frans Brueggen. The recorder is one of the easier instruments to master, but it takes a sense of musicianship and keen scholarship to rise to the top and dominate the field for as long as Brueggen has. To the recorder lover, you will buy it, and you will likely know exactly what you are getting.<br /><br />What if you listen occasionally to this music, and you're not entirely sure what a recorder is? Should you buy it? If you like to explore, perhaps you should, though the set has woefully few notes to guide you. Compare this set to the 3 LP set released in the early 70s of Brueggen playing 17 different recorders, where you got a treatise on the various instruments and how they were made, lavish photographs, and extensive backgrounds on the composers. You could and did learn a lot about early music from reading the notes back then. This set offers almost nothing of that, though all the pieces of that earlier set are included here. The beginner will get no clue about how to proceed. If you buy this set, you will need to use Wikipedia to find your way around.<br /><br />So unfortunately, this is a set for folks who already know about Brueggen and the recorder, a cheap digital dump. Call it a wasted opportunity.

0385349947|4.0|I bought the book after hearing Facebook COO Sheryl Sandberg in conversation with Google chairman Eric Schmidt at the Computer History Museum. I'd recommend this book for any career woman who wants to climb the \\"jungle gym\\", as Sandberg describes it, as opposed to a \\"corporate ladder\\".  Men should read this book to learn where they may fail women and what to do about it.<br /><br />She openly discusses her fears and luck in her successful career. She also mentions traits of other successful women. Being near Stanford University, she was able to tap some of the world's best researchers on gender inequalities.  The acknowledgement list is long and distinguished.  There are copious footnotes at the end of the book and references.  This book is most like the [[ASIN:0743222253 Living History]].<br /><br />Some of my values and experiences mirror those of Sheryl, some are quite different, in part because I went to a school and college for females only, so it's a book that lingers in your brain as you try to develop a strategic path for yourself.<br /><br />Here are some of the observations that Sheryl makes and actions she takes, backed up by research, and my thoughts:<br /> - more men than women want to be the President or have high goals - I found this surprising as at ten years old I thought I'd be on the steps of 10 Downing Street as Prime Minister in the UK<br /><br /> - women tend to be fearful in asking for promotions - I didn't have a clue that you were supposed to ask when I started work - I thought that like college and school you'd be promoted annually and that a boss's job was to make you a star - the only promotion I've had I asked for after advice from a senior manager - so the book is really valuable here in telling women how to get on in life<br /><br /> - Sheryl left a job at McKinsey after a year - I'd never have done that at her age as a core value above almost anything else for me was loyalty and I believed I could turn any situation into a good one (found out later this is not necessarily true)- - another valuable lesson<br /><br /> - people like to fit in - I've always wanted to be unique and differentiated from everyone around me - she tells a story about her son wearing a blue T-shirt on St Patrick's Day instead of green and her husband turning this into a lesson that it's OK not to be different - at least he wasn't wearing orange:)<br /><br /> - Sheryl urges people to take risks with their jobs - it depends on the risk - generally this is a good message, but it could spell disaster<br /><br /> - negotiate - good advice<br /><br /> - Sheryl worked 7:00 a.m. to 7:00 p.m. before she had a family - a good tip<br /><br /> - Sheryl tells women they can have a family and successful career - I'm not sure this is true for all<br /><br /> - most senior female CEOs have spouses - direct contrast to my school, where teachers without husbands were revered - even having a husband for me means distraction from career, at a girls' school I never learned at an early age to handle both work and dates<br /><br /> - B00CMT54DA|5.0|Matthew McConaughey has suffered for the last decade in romantic comedy hell with films like The Wedding Planner, Fool's Gold, Sahara and How To Lose a Guy In 10 Days with the only remotely decent films in that time being Frailty and Reign of Fire. How did he manage to climb out of acting mediocrity? Well we have to go back in time to the beginning of his career before we can go forward to the present and future. McConaughey struggled early in his career in the early and mid-1990's trying to find his footing as he took minor roles in films and one starring role in the dreadful Texas Chainsaw Massacre: The Next Generation. McConaughey finally got his big break in Joel Schumacher's adaptation of John Grisham's A Time to Kill starring as Jake Brigance taking the spot like from the likes of Samuel L. Jackson, Kevin Spacey, Sandra Bullock and Donald Sutherland. He embodied the Grisham's character so well that you can't imagine anyone else playing the character. From A Time to Kill he moved on to the small film Lone Star followed by Contact and Amistad showing off his acting capabilities and that is how he found redemption by making better film choices as he has chosen to work with great and good directors like Christopher Nolan in Interstellar and Martin Scorsese in The Wolf of Wall Street. It seems like McConaughey decided end his reputation as a pretty boy who can't act and return to his roots showing everyone how capable of an actor he which bring us to Mud.<br /><br />Mud is an intentionally sluggishly paced coming of age drama that follows the life and struggles of Ellis (Tye Sheridan) and the friends and family around him. Ellis and his best friend Neckbone (Jacob Lofland) live a river life on in southern Arkansas. The two find a boat resting high up in the trees on a small island on the river as if it is a sign from heaven the boys decided to make it their own only to find that someone has been living there. That is when they encounter a man simply known as Mud. Mud tells the boys he is waiting for someone and that if they boys help him get supplies he will then give them the boat. Through their journey with Mud we explore their hard lives as they deal with divorce, young love, facing the reality of the world and growing up. Jeff Nichols's screenplay is well written and thought out and inspired from Mark Twain. Yes his screenplay is slowly paced and for one moment in the film I wanted something to explode, but that was only a brief moment and otherwise I really enjoyed the story. The only flaw I had in the film was the climax at Ellis's boat home. It just seemed out of place, forced and didn't really work for me, but once the climax was over the epilogue was great.<br /><br />Reese Witherspoon like McConaughey seems to have spent most of her career in the romantic comedy world, but unlike McConaughey Witherspoon is good at it. She is charming, beautiful and has a good sense of humor, but it is nice to see her take a dramatic risk in Mud as Juniper the woman Mud loves since he was a child and has killed for. Reese shines in her supporting role displaying her charm, but in a more of a heartbreaking way. The Incomparable Sam Shepard does an outstanding job as the Tom Blankenship the only man Mud respects and the closest thing he has to a father. Michael Shannon the ever ready quality actor does a good job as Galen, Neckbone's uncle and the man who's raised him as he tries beautifully shows the nervousness yet desire to be more than a uncle to Neckbone, but a father. The rest of the cast from Sarah Paulson, Bonnie Sturdivant and to Ray McKinnon all do a good job in their roles and add something to the film.<br /><br />Mud is a naturally beautiful film. Filmed in Dumas and Stuttgart, Arkansas Adam Stone the cinematographer does a wonderful job showing off the natural beauty of the south and Arkansas which was matched by captivating minimalistic score done by composer David Wingo that fit the sluggish pace and cinematography of the film perfectly.<br /><br />While Mud isn't perfect story wise or pacing wise it still has a really good heartwarming coming of age story that is greatly complimented by the actors such as Matthew McConaughey in his best acting job of his career, Reese Witherspoon, and Tye Sheridan and Jacob Lofland who I think have a potential of a very bright career in Hollywood. That along with the cinematography and score make Mud a great film and one of the two best films I've seen this year along with Beyond the Pines. Though I don't think the pacing of the film will make it for everyone I still I highly recommend Mud before the deluge of the summer films starting next week.

B00BB22GQM|5.0|With hindsight we would do well to remember how many critics thought that Vampire Weekend's eponymous debut might be their first and last record. The \\"Upper West Side Soweto\\" template did seem to offer limited development opportunities and suspicions lingered that this was just a slice of novelty cultural tourism by (at best) a bunch of privileged preppy white boys and at worse four Ivy League bozo's. Their excellent sophomore album \\"Contra\\" started to suggest a band with much greater staying power who were moving into very interesting darker corners. Now their third album \\"Modern Vampires of the City\\" confirms a new album that is an undoubted keeper by a band who must now be ranked as best amongst their peer group. Repeated listens confirm an almost perfect pop album strong on infectious song-smithery, populated with hooks large enough to catch Tuna and lyrics so smart you can award an A+ diploma to each track. Yes MVOTC is essentially about New York but within its vinyl grooves are songs which tackle themes of faith, death and after-life albeit with a broad smille on their face. While at one time the band may have seen \\"the dawn in the colours of Bennetton\\" this superficiality has passed and the opener \\"Obvious bicycle\\" it is so beautifully melancholic that it could appear on a Mercury Rev album. The same applies to \\"The Unbelievers\\" which sounds like Buddy Holy's \\"Peggy Sue\\" rewritten for the 21st Century with a splash of world music thrown in for good measure. Ezra Koenig's skills are brilliantly honed on an album standout \\"Step\\" which is both a doo-wop track with harpsichord and shows that he has studied his Brian Wilson songbook well. It is a gorgeous song that cries out for the repeat button. The foot is further pressed down on the gas pedal for the buoyant single \\"Diane Young\\" surely destined to be the 'alternative' pop record of the summer? It is worth checking out the \\"making\\" of this song on the net which forms part of the excellent series of videos where the band enlist as their \\"Director\\" the actor Steve Buscemi to hit the streets to hustle New Yorkers into buying tickets to their Roseland Ballroom concert.<br /><br />Darker hues are fleshed out on the rubbery pop of \\"Everlasting Arms\\" a lush pounding song with a infectious drum undercurrent, wobbling bass and on repeated listens a rather funky sheen. Then we have the ubiquitous \\"Ya Heh\\" which has just about colonised every music blog for the last month with its regretful lyric of \\"America don't love you\\" and huge cartoon character backing to the infectious chorus and an ending to die for. Lookout in addition for \\"Hanna Hunt\\" which shows how Koenig's songwriting craft is maturing at warp speed not least the wistful line \\"If I can't trust you then damn it Hannah, there's no future, there's no answer.\\" The ultimate sign of a great pop band in this reviewers humble opinion is to construct a pop song at around two minutes and make it indelibly mark.. The 1.45 seconds of the last track \\"Young Lion\\" achieves this with a gentle rolling piano and melody that sticks like superglue. Granted it is still early days with \\"Modern Vampires\\" and one of the longest tracks on the record \\"Hudson\\" has yet to really land a blow. This is countered by the sheer fun and outrageous audacity of \\"Finger love\\" which could happily grace an Animal Collective album and fit like a glove. The spoken interlude in the song is followed by Keonig recalling \\"Remembrances of holy days in Tarrytown and Rye/I don't wanna live like this, but I don't wanna die\\". Finally \\"Worship you\\" reminds you of the theme tune for the old series Bonanza, surely always a great sign?<br /><br />While \\"Modern Vampires of the City\\" may not be the best album of 2013 (time will tell) it is by a country mile the most enjoyable listening experience of the year thus far. It is a life affirming album by a band entering their prime. The good news is that any doubt around the longevity of Vampire Weekend is dispelled by this record. This fact alone should be a cause of celebration. A while ago one Amazon reviewer had the audacity to describe Vampire Weekend as the new Talking Heads. They are two very different beasts but in the context of this new album we get a huge pot pourri of whimsical, esoteric lyrics combined with a mix of elements of punk rock, art rock, avant-garde, pop, funk, world music, and Americana. Does this sound familiar? Whatever the case \\"Modern Vampires\\" is a completely joyful album which you are going to want to own pronto.. This fact alone should be a cause of celebration. They are two very different beasts but in the context of this new album we get a huge pot pourri of whimsical, esoteric lyrics combined with a mix of elements of punk rock, art rock, avant-garde, pop, funk, world music, and Americana. Does this sound familiar? Whatever the case \\"Modern Vampires\\" is a completely joyful album which you are going to want to own pronto.

B000URWYTS|5.0|This is a very engaging and revealing book about the love affair between Frank Lloyd Wright and Mumma Borthwick Cheney, while he was still married to his first wife, Catherine.  The reader is pulled into the Victorian era where women's rights were severely limited and where women's identities was almost totally entwined with that of their husbands and children. The frustration and thoughts of a brilliant woman, who was way ahead of her time, is brought to life by this author who skillfully and cleverly filled in the gaps of time by using documented information, even though some details appeared to be scarce.  The book mentions journal's that were kept by Mumma Bothwick Cheney, but it is difficult to determine if they survived. Mumma appeared to struggle with every aspect of her position as the live-in lover of a great, very public man, who was significantly eccentric in his own right.  She did love him, though; thus, her own tremendous personal sacrifices and the revealing title of the book. One will never know what would have happened between them as time went on, if a tragedy had not occurred that ended her life. History shows that Frank did marry two other women after divorcing his first wife, following Mumma's death.  The book is well written and organized with details of other real-life characters, the houses that Frank designed and definitely engages the reader from beginning to end.

B0090SI4OC|2.0|How do you turn a 619 page bestseller into a movie? Andrew Niccol had the challenge of directing the adaptation of Stephenie Meyer's The Host. I have been a huge fan of The Host since I read it in May 2012 and I had such high expectations for this movie. I was pretty disappointed with the film adaptation of The Host.<br /><br />  The Host starts off with a bang and immediately takes viewers into the complex world of the Souls and Hosts. Saoirse Ronan is a rebel named Melanie who gets captured by the Souls and she becomes a Host to Wanderer (Wanda). Wanda's job is to find Melanie's brother (Chandler Canterbury) and Melanie's boyfriend, Jared (Max Irons). Wanda is not prepared for the troubles of being human. Wanda meets up with Jamie and Jared but not everyone is happy to see her. Ian (Jake Abel) soons become entangled in Melanie's life.<br /><br />  The Host is pretty true to it's source material, there are only a few differences between the novel and movie. The problem is not with the concept of the movie but how The Host is executed. I feel like the cast members of The Host lacked passion for their characters.<br /><br />  A majority of the acting in The Host lacks the raw and powerful emotion found in the novel. Saoirse Ronan does a decent job at playing both Melanie and Wanda. I didn't really feel any empathy toward Melanie and Wanda when I saw the movie. When I read The Host, both characters really struck a chord with me emotionally. Both love interests Jared and Ian weren't exactly convincing in portraying their characters. I didn't feel like Ronan had any chemistry with Irons or Abel, the romance just didn't feel existent. Sure, Ronan kept locking lips with Irons and Abel but I didn't think their characters true felt any love towards each other.<br /><br />  I think the most exceptional performances are carried out by Diane Kruger and William Hurt. Kruger's portrayal of The Seeker was extremely terrifying and powerful. Every single time I saw Kruger character, I was ready for a fabulous performance. William Hurt was the best choice for Jeb and his portrayal of Jeb was simply perfect. Hurt shows so many different angles of his character and his performance truly captured the essence of Jeb's character.<br /><br />  Plotwise The Host moves at a ridiculously slow pace and I think the movie should have been edited better. The transitions between each scene were not smooth and were a bit on the awkward side. The Host is about what it means to be human and yet this message seemed to be lost in this adaptation. There was none of the powerful messages and motifs that I remember reading about it Meyer's novel.<br /><br />  Also lost in translation to the big screen was Jared and Ian. Jared and Ian were huge parts of the novel and yet in the movie they didn't feel so significant. The \\"love box\\" (4 love interests) felt extremely fake because the viewer can't connect with any of the main characters. Jake Abel's performance is extremely unconvincing, he transitions almost instantaneously from wanting to kill Wanda to falling for her. I expected to see Abel slowly fall in love with Wanda but this was not the case. The dialogues between Wanda and Jared and Wanda and Ian were extremely memorable in the novel. This movie adaptation doesn't feature 95% of my favorite quotes from the novel.<br /><br />  I seem to focus on what I didn't like but there were many things I really liked about The Host. I loved the way all of the futuristic technology was shown in The Host. Also all of the action scenes were extremely intense and well done.  I would love to see more of the action scenes because I really enjoyed watching them.It was interesting to see how the makers of The Host's interpretation of Souls. Though I complained about the acting in this film, the acting wasn't terrible.<br /><br />  The Host is a mediocre movie ruined by unconvincing acting, an extremely slow pace, and badly written script. While watching The Host I was extremely antsy and I feel like this movie could have been a half hour shorter. I know all of the cast members have expressed interests in making The Host a franchise. I would definitely see a sequel but I would probably wait till it gets released on DVD.  I think I would have enjoyed this movie more if I hadn't read the novel. The novel is far better than the movie and I would recommending reading the book instead. The Host wasn't the worst movie I have seen but it certainly wasn't the best.sy and I feel like this movie could have been a half hour shorter. I know all of the cast members have expressed interests in making The Host a franchise. I would definitely see a sequel but I would probably wait till it gets released on DVD. The Host wasn't the worst movie I have seen but it certainly wasn't the best.

B00AFZ86BA|3.0|I first learned about this release via an announcement on Facebook.  Various online sources stated that Ratt Original Album Series (2013) contained remastered versions of the band's first five albums, [[ASIN:B001HADE00 Out Of The Cellar]] (1984), [[ASIN:B001AUKUS6 Invasion Of Your Privacy]] (1985), [[ASIN:B000002IJO Dancing Undercover]] (1986), [[ASIN:B001G9LV5M Reach For The Sky]] (1988), and [[ASIN:B000002IPR Detonator]] (1990).  Although I already owned all of these albums, based on them being remastered, I ordered the \\"boxed set\\", which consists of a cardboard outer box, housing the albums which are protected by cardboard sleeves that replicate the original album covers.<br /><br />Looking through the set, the word \\"remastered\\" is nowhere to be found.  Released and \\"marketed\\" by Rhino, the discs have a different look from the original Atlantic Records releases, but still have the Atlantic Records emblem.  Rather than reflecting a 2013 copyright, each CD has the original release date, and none of the albums contain any bonus tracks.  Informally comparing the sound of the \\"old\\" albums versus the \\"new\\" releases, I am unable to discern a significant difference or improvement, in audio quality.  I could be mistaken, but this set seems to be just a repackaging by Rhino, of the original Atlantic recordings.  While this set is a nice deal for those who do not have all the albums, for those like me who already owned them, it's disappointing to discover that I have scammed into a rebuy.<br /><br />Ratt is a band whose sound seemed to evolve from album to album, with a significant transition occurring on Reach For The Sky, which was slicker, cleaner, and more heavily produced than those preceding it.  I think that Ratt's first three albums, Dancing Undercover in particular, could have really benefitted from remastering, to achieve a bigger, fuller, more impactful sound.<br /><br />If you're a newcomer, looking to get hard and heavy into Ratt, this is a convenient way to do so.  In the mid-80's, Ratt was at the forefront of the LA metal scene, and these five albums, featuring the classic lineup of Steven Pearcy, Warren DiMartini, the late Robbin Crosby, Juan Croucier, and Bobby Blotzer, represent the band at the peak of their popularity.  As noted in the product description, this is an import item.  I purchased my copy at Amazon.UK for about $18.  Shipping was combined with items in my order, which probably added a dollar or so.<br /><br />If you already own the original Atlantic releases, there is little reason to look at this collection.  A surprising number of artists have had part of their catalog reissued under the \\"Original Album Series\\" banner, which pricewise are generally good deals.  Do a search, and you might be surprised how many bands and artists, from a variety of genres, appear.  I haven't done much research, but there is probably little or no remastered material involved in these compilations.so. The muted colors matched the often bleak landscape of the Bronx.  I was born there and remember it well.  Mbps rate averages in the low 20's but, never the less, the video is as clean a transfer as it needs to be.<br /><br />AUDEO...In many ways, the audio is the star of the Blu Ray presentation.  The disc defaults to a lossless DTS HD 5.1 and the sound editors were given full liberty, I assume, to do a masterful job of audio steering.  There are a great many pans between the front and rear sound stages when appropriate as well as a plentiful and enveloping soundscape throughout the film.  The directional use of the rear and front side speakers come in to use very often as needed and this discreet channeling of audio really made the film come alive for me.  That the audio is lossless only makes for more transparent and realistic sound as it does here.  It can be easy to lose awareness of the full home theater's use but I am always conscious of well done audio as The American Gangster on Blu Ray does.<br /><br />Extras...A couple of deleted scenes, an alternate opening and pretty much all the extras found on the original Standard Def release.<br /><br />All my reviews focus solely on the quality of the transfers to Blu Ray of both video and audio and I do hope that this review has been of some help to you in deciding upon your purchase decisions. B003ZHVJFA|3.0|Well, I've got to start this review by saying that this movie is a total guilty pleasure.  Tekken certainly isn't a good movie and it is by no means the film adaptation of the hit video game series that Tekken fans everywhere would have loved to see.  Fighting games are known for having convoluted and awkward storylines and its nice to see that they didn't really bother with that here.  Beyond having the basics of the Mishima-Kazama clan down and how they're all related to each other, the movie doesn't take a whole lot from the games.  The people who made the film evidently at least watched footage of the series however, since they seem to realize its all about fighting.<br /><br />Aesthetically, the movie has a lot in common with the dystopian sci-fi flicks of the late 80s and early 90s and like movies with names like Escape From New York, the quality of the acting is about the same.  There's some competence from the leads but the supports and backups are all dreadful.  Tekken is by no means oscar-worthy material but if you are able to divorce it from the inconsistencies with the story in the game franchise, it is a pretty decent popcorn flick.  The film quality is excellent and some of the fights are very good.  The film doesn't demonstrate a lot of real world logic and I'm not sure if that's because they couldn't be bothered to write good dialogue or if they realized the dialogue was just filler in between the fight scenes but either way there's a lot of (usually unintentional) humor in between the fight scenes.  I was definitely entertained by the movie however.  I'd give it a solid thumbs up to anyone who is into cheese and over-the-top action with a touch of overacting.  If you're a serious fan of the game franchise however, it's probably a solid avoid since they definitely didn't bother to familiarize themselves with the actual storyline from the games.

B005LAII62|4.0|PARANORMAN has caused a certain degree of controversy among parents in search of children's fare, so you should know upfront that this is not a movie that pre-schoolers will take kindly.  Young children are likely to find it too frightening and the references obscure.  It's really more of a pre-teen-and-up sort of flick.  That said, it's a fun, unpretentious little movie with some of the best stop-motion animation I've seen in a long time, and while I wouldn't put it in the same league as THE NIGHTMARE BEFORE CHRISTMAS, I wouldn't mind seeing a couple of times more.<br /><br />The story focuses on bullying, a deservedly hot topic.  Norman Babcock is your average kid except for one thing: he can see and talk to dead people.  His mother attempts to be understanding a new-agey sort of way; his father is in explosive disbelief; his teen-age, cell-phone-addicted sister Courtney finds the whole thing embarassing.  But there is one person who believes him: his peculiar Uncle Prederghast, who believes it because he can do it himself.  For most of life Uncle Prederghast has been guarding a secret, and it has something to do with the town's history, which includes an unsavory story of a dangerous witch, a curse, and the living dead.  But Uncle Prederghast is getting old, and he's eager to pass on responsibility to Norman.<br /><br />Throughout the film characters bully each other frequently--but then find themselves in the wrong, and most often discover their bullying is based on a fear of what they do not understand.  Of particular interest is the notion that bullying is something that is passed along: you bully me, I bully the next kid, and so on.  This being a movie (as opposed to real life), all the characters (including zombies and the evil witch) learn to break the cycle.  Along the way we have references to SCOOBY-DOOBY-DOO (those meddling kids!), MARS ATTACKS (the scurrying hand), HALLOWEEN (cell phone music), and FRIDAY THE 13TH (hockey mask.)  A lot of the fun of the film is in recognizing these, so \\"movie nerds\\" are likely to enjoy it more than others will.<br /><br />The movie was originally released in 3D on the big screen, and I suspect it played better in that format than it does on DVD.  Whatever the case, the DVD comes with several entertaining extras.  Recommended.<br /><br />GFT, Amazon Reviewere film is in recognizing these, so \\"movie nerds\\" are likely to enjoy it more than others will.<br /><br />The movie was originally released in 3D on the big screen, and I suspect it played better in that format than it does on DVD. B00B7364H2|5.0|I ended my review of volume 1, [[ASIN:B005XBA9Y8 Mercury Living Presence Boxed Set]], with the words &#34;Now, when does volume 2 with 50 more come out?&#34; Well, it turns out to be 55, and here it is. For those who'd have inadvertently landed here from a long sojourn on planet Mars, let them be reminded that Mercury was a label established in 1945 at the end of the 78s era (a division of Mercury Radio and Television Corporation), first devoted to and famed for its catalog of popular music and jazz, but that branched out to classical at the end of the 1940s and became a prized audiophile series in the LP era under its moniker &#34;Living Presence&#34; and under the aegis of C. Robert Fine (sound engineer) and Wilma Cozart (producer) ; in fact the Fine-Cozart team worked so well together that they eventually became Mr and Mrs Fine, in 1957. Others were involved too, as David Hall the producer of the early releases, Harold Lawrence, producer of the late releases, Robert Eberenz, Fine's erstwhile associate engineer who became sole in charge starting in 1964, and George Piros, in charge of the delicate processing of the master tapes onto state-of-the-art LPs. I urge anybody interested in knowing what kind or recording techniques made the Mercury Living Presence recordings so special to go read the Wikipedia entry on Mercury Records, section &#34;Mercury Living Presence Series&#34;, and the stupendous online article, essay, paean, monument by Rudolf A. Bruil published on soundfountain, both of which they can complement with the online interview of Wilma Cozart Fine by Bruce Duffie from 1995 on kcstudio dot com and the 1999 interview of Robert Eberenz on opusklassiek. Don't miss, also, for the complete Mercury catalog, LPs and CDs, with all the cover photos, on mercury dot lacyway dot com, another jaw-dropping monument to the label.<br /><br />The Mercury Living Presence audiophile cachet was rejuvenated in the CD era, thanks to the careful and loving remasterings made in the years 1990 to 1995 by Wilma Cozart Fine (who passed away in 2009, aged 82), always based on the masters and using the restored &#34;original Mercury vacuum-type Westrex film recorder and Amex 3-track tape machine&#34;, with &#34;no equalization, filtering, compression or limiting&#34; (the few SACD releases were not done by Mrs Cozart Fine and did not use the restored equipment, but Revox machines). There were 136 CDs reissues (some in 2- or 3-CD sets), excluding two compilations of samples (&#34;You Are There!&#34; volume 1 & 2). Each CD usually reissued the content of one and a half LP, sometimes two, many compiled material from various LPs and recording sessions, always into very coherent and attractive programs, and most were filled to the brim. Volume 1 and now volume 2 collect those original CDs as they were, e.g. with no reshuffling of content. I have too many of the individual releases to justify buying the compilation sets, but from reviews and comments of the previous one, it appears that it came with only limited information about precise dating of the recordings and the technical info provided in the booklets of the individual CDs (and none of the original liner notes). Not so with the new issue: it offers a great booklet (I was able to consult it), with the detailed technical information about dates, personnel and microphones, a long essay of Mike Gray on the history of &#34;Living Presence&#34; with loads of more fascinating technical info, followed by an article reprinted from the July/August 2012 issue of TapeOp by Thomas Fine, the son of Robert and Wilma, and on top of that dozens after dozens of great photos.<br /><br />It would seem then that the new set needs no recommendation - no more than the previous one, seeing how fast it went -, just a detailed listing of its contents. But it turns out that the set offers a bonus, which calls for special and extensive comment, as it raises the issue of what it is that truly DEFINES &#34;Mercury Living Presence&#34;; for reasons of length and Amazon strictures I have sent it to the comments section.<br /><br />There are too many CDs for me to provide the product links to the original CD releases, but I've added the label numbers and ASIN numbers, which should make it easy to go to the entry of the original CD. The recording dates I provide are collated from various sources: the individual CDs when I have them, but also the mammoth Mercury discography compiled by Michel Ruppli and Ed Novitsky (volume IV has the chronological listing of the Mercury Living Presence classical records, [[ASIN:0313290342 The Mercury Labels: A Discography Volume IV The 1969-1991 Era and Classical Recordings (Discographies)]]), and the discography of the London Symphony Orchestra by Philip Stuart (it is now hosted on the website of CHARM, the Research Center for the History and Analysis of Recorded Music). I have found no discrepancy between Stuart and Ruppli/Novitsky; there are a few (but, except in a few cases, never more serious than a few days) between them and the dating provided on the CDs. In some cases where I've been able to check, the booklets were wrong and the Ruppli/Novitsky dating was right (no way Mercury could have been recording Paray's Auber Overtures in Detroit on April 4, 1959, AND Dorati's Petrouchka in Minneapolis. Ruppli/Novitsky's 20 April for the latter is confirmed by other sources), but in another case (Janis' Pictures at an Exhibition), it was the other way around.<br /><br />CD 1: Originally 432 006. Schoenberg: 5 Pieces For Orchestra, Op.16 (19 & 20 July 1962); Webern: 5 Pieces For Orchestra, Op.10 (22 July 1962); Berg: Three Pieces For Orchestra, Op.6 (14 & 21 July 1962), Lulu Suite (Helga Pilarczyk, 19-24 June 1961). Hilde Somer, San Antonio SO, Victor Alessandro. 1:07:52<br /><br />The logic is clear: successively Dorati (not quite 20 CDs, since one is shared with Kubelik and one and a half has Fistoulari's Sylvia), Marcel Dupr&eacute; (1 disc), Fennell (9 CDs and thank God not just marches, marches and marches this time), Hanson (10 CDs), Kubelik (1 CD plus the half-one shared with Dorati), Paray (8 CDs), Puyana (3 CDs), Starker (1 CD). But here comes the throw-in. CD 54, Dorati again, and with only The Rite of Spring, for a stingy 31 minutes? And anyway Dorati's Minneapolis Rite was already on volume 1, so what's the deal here?<br /><br />Well here's the deal. On volume 1 was Dorati's stereo remake from 15 November 1959, here you get his mono recording from 12-14 December 1953.<br /><br />And then, CD 55: Corigliano's Piano Concerto and Strauss' Parergon, plus a 16-minute interview of Corigliano. Now THAT'S interesting, and quite a bonus too. Neither the early Dorati Rite nor the Corigliano/Strauss program were ever part of the batch of CDs reissued under the auspices of Wilma Cozart Fine in the early 1990s. THEY ARE NEW TO CD. As for the interview, it was never before issued commercially. The booklet indicates that it &#34;was originally released as a promotional LP by Mercury's marketing department&#34;.<br /><br />But the inclusion of the Corigliano/Strauss program, as exciting news as it is, also raises a load of questions. The recordings were made on May 19 & 20, 1969 and it is not clear that they legitimately belong to the &#34;Living Presence&#34; Series. For (much) more on that and the justification of this review's title, please go to the comments section, starting part II.<br /><br />A question that some readers and fans of Mercury might ask themselves: with these two volumes of reissues totaling 50+53 CDs (not counting the two new discs of the new set), which ones, among the Wilma Cozart Fine reissued CDs, are STILL left out?<br /><br />Well, more than a handful - which makes me hope that a volume 3 will eventually come out. The original CD reissue of Hanson's Merry Mount Suite, Mosaics and Piano Concerto (vol. 1 CD 8) came with a second CD of explanations by the composer, which wasn't included in the volume 1 compilation, and I guess this one will be left out forever. Which, from Mercury's original batch of 136, leaves 32 to go (135 - 50 - 53). For the complete list, see also the comments section (Part I).<br /><br />Now, 32 CDs would seem stingy compared to the 51 and 55 of the first two volumes. Does it mean that enough new remasterings and releases to fill out 20 more CDs are in store? I sure do hope so. The Living Presence catalog was fairly extensively covered by the Wilma Cozart Fine reissues, at least the stereo era, but even there, there were some frustrating omissions. As for the mono era, it was largely left untapped, even considering that it was relatively short (stereo recording began at the end of 1955) and that much was later re-made in stereo. Among the gold nuggets still to be mined: Kubelik's early Tchaikovsky, Brahms and Bloch with Chicago, Hanson's series of American music including symphonies of Barber, Harris, Cowell, Piston and his own 4th and 5th, Paray's mono and stereo Beethoven and Brahms' 4th from 1955 mono, Dorati's Beethoven Symphonies No. 4 & 8 from November 1955 (Mercury Living Presence's first recording in stereo, but apparently the stereo tapes are lost) and his 1957 &#34;Eroica&#34;, complete Tchaikovsky Sleeping Beauty (1955 mono), 1953 Copland 3rd Symphony, 1963 Schumann Fourth, his abundant Haydn (Symphony No. 45 &#34;Farewell&#34;, &#34;Fire&#34;-59, No. 81, 94 &#34;Surprise&#34;, 100 &#34;Miltary&#34;, 101 &#34;Clock&#34; and 103 &#34;Drumroll&#34; were recorded between 1957 and 1965). And add to all this a number of recordings not usually associated with Mercury but indeed recorded by Fine and the Mercury team and thus deserving inclusion (see the discussion in the comments section for more about this), like Tchaikovsky's Violin Concerto with Szeryng and the LSO from July 1962 and Beethoven's Violin Concerto with the same and Hans Schmidt-Isserstedt from July 1965. There's also Josef Szigeti's final batch of recordings, made from 1959 to 1961, even if the once great violinist is only the painful shadow of himself. A number of these have already been reissued on CD, but in Japan only and they need to be made available in the West, preferably in transfers using Wilma Cozart Fine's restored Mercury machines. For a complete and I hope tantalizing list, see again the comments section, starting Part IV. Interviewed by Bruce Duffie in 1995, as she was still working on her program of reissues, to the question &#34;will there eventually be a time when everything in the original catalog is back out?&#34;, Wilma Cozart Fine said this: &#34;We're certainly aiming in that direction. We may run into trouble with certain ones due to deterioration or the condition of the tapes. The truth of the matter is, as with all recording projects we will release them as long as we have them and as long as the public wants them and buys them, then we will continue to go through the catalog.&#34;<br /><br />The success of volume 1 seems to give a clear answer to that, as should the success of volume 2. Indeed, potential buyer, act quick. When I published my review of vol. 1, it sold at a little less than 3 a disc. Now? More than 9 and trending to 10, no more a bargain - and you are lucky if it is sold at all: last time I checked (early March), no more offer on the French sister company,only one (and expensive) offer on the UK and German websites. For the latecomers, remains eBay and patience, or impatience and burglary at their local library.ikimora op. 63 (Eastman Philharmonia, Hanson 8 May 1962); Charles Martin Loeffler: Two Rapsodies (soloists Robert Sprenkle oboe, Francis Tursi viola, Armand Basile piano, 6 May 1958); Howard Hanson: Fantasy Variations on a Theme of Youth (soloist David Burge, 7 May 1956). 0143038583|5.0|This book is full of interesting information. For example, Pollan shows how portion sizes in fast foods have increased over the decades. It is much easier, for example, to get a customer to buy one newly-larger portion of fries than to get him/her to buy two equivalent erstwhile smaller portions.<br /><br />The author clarifies the matter of high-fructose corn syrup. Whether or not it is the same as sugar, in terms of effects on the body, is not the issue. The issue is the indiscriminate way it has been added to so many foods that had not been sweetened before.<br /><br />Pollan has a fascinating account of Joel Salatin and his traditional farm at Polyface, Virginia. The example of sustainable farming is essentially an enclosed ecosystem. The contrasts with agribusiness-based farms are along these lines: ecological, not industrial; perennial species, not annual species (such as corn); diverse crop species, not monoculture; solar energy, not fossil fuels; local market, not global market; local fertility, not imported fertility. Cows graze on pasture, and the locations are rotated so that the grass is not overeaten. Chickens engage in their natural habits of eaten larvae from cow pats. They are enclosed in a bottomless enclosure that is moved ten feet every day. That way, no grassy area is overwhelmed by the nitrogen from their feces.<br /><br />Finally, the author provides a series of websites. These describe where one can buy local, truly organic produce. (Otherwise, be careful because the term \\"organic\\" is a relatively amorphous term).

B005LAII62|3.0|The second half of 2012 saw four big name animated films released on the big screen.  One of them, Wreck-It Ralph, ran away at the box office and was loved by critics.  The other three, Frankenweenie, ParaNorman, and Hotel Transylvania, battled for second place.  All four films had great animation, good casting, a good blend of action, comedy, and drama, and a solid advertising campaign preceding their American releases.  All four films also had un-original origins in that they all drew heavily from prior animated blockbusters.  So why the sharp difference in performance?  The answer to this is key to understanding costumer choices, and the ability to distinguish oneself from their competitors.  Wreck-It Ralph stood out as it was the only film about video games, and featured an antihero in the lead role.  The other three films seemed eerily similar in that their plots delved into the world of the supernatural - horror fantasy genres, and featured child characters in the lead roles.  Frankenweenie is basically Frankenstein with a dog instead of a man, and a boy instead of a doctor.  ParaNorman was an animated remake of Sixth Sense blended with plot-lines from other horror films.  And Hotel Transylvania drew upon elements of multiple horror films.  Its as if most of Hollywood shared the same scripts and screenplays, but just changed up the casting and animation style.  This is unfortunate, as the latter three were solid films, and each would have performed much better  in the absence of the other two.<br /><br />Now my review for ParaNorman.  The plot line seems like a hybrid of Sixth Sense, and a multitude of films about witches, such as Lords of Salem.  The pace of the movie was fairly slow, with none of the manic action and snappy dialogue common to other animated films.  The soundtrack matched the pace of the movie.  The plot logic was sound, with great attention to detail.  The animation was believable though nothing spectacular.  There were some scary scenes inthe film that toddlers would probably get nightmares from, so I would not bring anyone under 5 to see this film.  Overall, a film that is above average, even borderline good.  Except when stacked up against the competitors that Hollywood put out last year, it failed to stand out...

0812993845|4.0|Wedding Night is another fabulous addition to Sophie Kinsella's chick lit novels. Before I start this review I just have to point out that I am a huge fan of Sophie Kinsella. She was the one that helped me through my high school years, by her ever hilarious books, from the Shopaholic series, Can You Keep a Secret? to Remember Me? Of course even her recent releases have become some of my favorite chick lits, specifically I've Got Your Number which I actually reviewed on this blog last year. You can imagine how excited I was to find out she has a new novel with her trademark protagonist and a laughter inducing plot. I can tell a Sophie Kinsella female protagonist a mile away. There is this unique trait I find in every one, they are british of course, always are the center of the most embarrassing situations but are still so endearing and very likable. Half of the time I tend to always yell \\"oh no! can't you see this is a DISASTROUS idea?\\" I mean if you haven't read her books, I'm pretty sure you at least watched the Shopaholic movie, so you must know what I mean.<br />Back to Wedding Night. What is different about this book is that it is actually told from two POVs, something Sophie Kinsella has never done before. I was, to be honest, a bit wary, especially since the second POV belongs to the main protagonist's older sister. I even started reading it back in february but stopped after 40 pages, excitement deflated and actually surrendering a little bit that I might actually end up not liking a Sophie Kinsella book.. something that is still unheard of. However when I picked it up again in March, I must say that I got sucked in after the initial slow start. What was funny is that in the end I enjoyed the POV of the older sister, Fliss, way more than the main protagonist, Lottie. Fliss was the level headed one of the two. Lottie got swept up in the unrealistic romance, getting proposed by a guy she hasn't seen for 15 years. The guy she had a romantic couple of weeks with in Ikonos, a greek island back when she was 18. Fliss tried warning her, and even started creating elaborate schemes to try and stop this sudden and crazy marriage. By this time I was more interested in Fliss and the sudden appearance of Lorcan, Ben's old friend. Lottie and Ben's relationship felt too rushed (obviously! Being married like that). Fliss on the other hand... I have to say Lorcan is the type of guy I crave for in Kinsella's books. The brooding, director of a big shot company with the 10 thousand dollar suits and serious, don't mess with me, face. He reminds me of both men from I've Got Your Number and Shopaholic, but is still his own man. He was a character I wanted to know more about and actually got to do just that. Fliss and Lorcan's scenes, especially with Richard, Lottie's ex-boyfriend, and Fliss's young son, Noah, who starts telling the most convoluted lies to people, such as him getting heart surgery among others that crack any readers up.<br />This wouldn't be a Sophie Kinsella novel without the fun and hilarious banter as well as the awkward and embarrassing situations. Fliss trying to keep Lottie and Ben from cashing in on their wedding night as well as trying to teach Noah that it is wrong to tell lies like that, especially when they come back and bite Fliss back in the butt. As for the writing, it was superb, I didn't expect any less from Sophie Kinsella. The ending was actually quite surprising but refreshing. That is what I like about Sophie Kinsella, she takes overused chick lit plots and transforms them into original and fantastic plots. Now I must say I am a bit bummed out and the reason is because I have to wait another full year for the next novel by Sophie Kinsella. I might pick up some of her previous ones to pass the time. I seriously recommend Wedding Night to chick lit lovers as well as YA novel readers who want to branch out their usual reading genres.in Ikonos, a greek island back when she was 18. This may not be one of them.<br /><br />\\"Cloud Atlas\\" may not be your type of film but if it is and it intrigues you enough, you'll find yourself drawn back to watch it more than once because of the nonlinear storytelling which thematically links six different periods of time from the past, present to the future. The theme driving the film is that our lives have a ripple effect through time and that our souls continue to reappear with a chance of absolution for past mistakes. In some cases the characters (those played by Hugo Weaving and Hugh Grant for example) continually get it wrong and grow worse with each incarnation.<br /><br />As others have pointed out \\"Cloud Atlas\\" is much like [[ASIN:B008ROF4VG The Fountain [HD]]] except that the editing of the film links each era based on actions, events and what happens to the characters in each given year. The stories take place in the following times/places: 1849 in the South Pacific as a lawyer tries to broker a slave deal; England and Scotland in 1936 as a gay composer struggles to complete his own masterpiece; San Francisco, CA 1978 focusing ona reporter trying to break a story about a dangerous power plant who's life is in danger; England in 2012 as a publisher gets an unexpected windfall when his author (a former thug)kills a critic; the year 2144 in Neo Seoul (Korea) as a \\"Fabricant\\" (an artifically engineer person) is interviewed about leading a revolution prior to her execution; the big island in an undisclosed far future where Earth has fallen into two camps--one advanced and the other primative struggling to survive on what's left of our world.<br /><br />The Blu-ray is a very nice transfer of the film. Each period has a unique look to it in terms of the photography and color scheme. We also get a lossless 5.1 audio mix as well as Spanish and French tracks. I first encountered it in a theater, then purchased the VHS tape.  I would have bought the earlier DVD, except that reviewers widely panned the sound.  The French film company Gaumont issued it on Blu-ray on 2010, but in a solely European format.  Finally, Olive Films has released a Blu-ray version for U.S. viewers (as well as a new, presumably improved, DVD).<br /><br />Although filmed operas always raise some objections, I've never found another version of Mozart's masterpiece that I've enjoyed as much.  (My favorite live production stars Bryn Terfel from the Met.)  The Vicenza settings are spectacular--especially the final consuming fire--and  cannot be equaled on any opera house stage.  Ruggero Raimondi makes a most believable Don, and the other members of the cast also shine both as singers and actors.  Lorin Maazel conducts the orchestra and chorus of the Paris Opera in a fine performance.  The quality of the audio and video are very good, if not fully equal to those of a 21st century recording.<br /><br />I don't know if Olive's Blu-ray transfer is the same as Gaumont's, although it really doesn't matter, since the latter can't be viewed on U.S. players anyway.  Gaumont also included a disc of extras, including documentaries on making the original film and on remixing it for Blu-ray.  Olive gives us only the film, with optional English subtitles--but that's enough.
